Extended data figures and tables
Extended Data Fig. 1 Single nucleotide polymorphism patterns across pairwise genome alignments reflect point mutations and recombination.
a. Illustration of two types of recombination. For the focal pair of genomes (G1 and G2, dark cyan), recombination can happen between the two genomes (homogenizing recombination, light blue), or with phylogenetically more distant genomes (diversifying recombination, red). b. Recombination alters the SNP distribution between two genomes by either eliminating SNPs (homogenizing recombination, light blue) or by increasing the SNP density (diversifying recombination, red). The distribution of the number of SNPs in sliding windows across pairwise genome alignments can be expressed as the fractional sum of a Poisson distribution (point mutations), and a Negative Binomial distribution that accounts for both diversifying and homogenizing recombination.
Extended Data Fig. 2 Validation of the mixture model for recombination estimation with simulated data.
a. Phylogenetic structure of sets of 64 simulated genomes evolved along a phylogenetic tree with sweep clusters under 144 parameter combinations (Methods). b. Comparison of the recombined genome fraction in the simulation and estimated by the model. Different colors represent categories of genome pairs where the model accurately estimates the recombined genome fractions (dark blue), or is insufficient due to different reasons: yellow, insufficient differentiation of the clonal and recombined fragments due to similar divergences (< 2.5-fold difference); grey, overall divergence too low (< 0.0075%); red, insufficient length of recombination fragments (exponential distribution with a mean of 200 base pairs). c-f. Comparison between simulated vs. estimated values within the functional range of the model for recombined genome fraction, clonal divergence, the effective recombination rate r/m, and relative recombination to mutation occurrence rate rho/theta. g-l. same as a-f but for sets of 64 simulated genomes with balanced phylogenetic tree structure.
Extended Data Fig. 3 Population genomic signatures of genome-wide selective sweep clusters.
Histogram of the Tajima’s D for all GWSS clusters detected in commensal gut bacteria. ***P < 0.001, **P < 0.01, and *P < 0.1. Significance was assessed using a two-sided parametric test of deviation from the standard neutral model using the beta approximation of the sampling distribution of D.
Extended Data Fig. 4 Average SNP distance within genome-wide selective sweep clusters for commensal, pathogenic and probiotic bacteria.
Histograms show the distribution of average SNP distances within GWSS clusters for each bacterial category, with the dashed line indicating the mean of the distribution. See Methods for how SGBs are assigned to the three different categories.
Extended Data Fig. 5 Comparison of genome-wide sweep clusters identified using isolate-based clonal frames and StrainPhlAn marker genes for each of the 46 commensal SGBs.
a. The probability that samples (including isolate genomes and metagenomes) within GWSS clusters identified using isolate-based clonal frames also fall within sweep clusters called from the StrainPhlAn marker gene trees. b. The probability that samples (including isolate genomes and metagenomes) within sweep clusters called from the StrainPhlAn marker gene trees, also fall within GWSS clusters identified using isolate-based clonal frames.
Extended Data Fig. 6 Stacked bar plots summarizing host conditions associated with GWSS clusters in all commensal SGBs.
Each row represents a GWSS cluster within a SGB and is labeled according to the major host condition(s) associated with that cluster (Padj < 0.05, no regional bias). Associations were tested using general linear models. All tests were two-sided, and P values were adjusted using the Benjamini–Hochberg false discovery rate (FDR) procedure. Regional bias was assessed using two-sided chi-squared goodness-of-fit tests within each sweep; sweeps with P < 0.05 were classified as regionally biased. Within each row, the length of each colored segment represents the enrichment of samples from different host conditions in a given GWSS cluster, relative to their occurrences in the entire SGB, with segment colors denoting the respective conditions.
Extended Data Fig. 7 SGBs associated with host conditions.
Heatmaps of statistically significant (Padj < 0.05) associations between entire SGBs and host conditions. Associations were tested using general linear models. All tests were two-sided, and P values were adjusted using the Benjamini–Hochberg false discovery rate (FDR) procedure.
Extended Data Fig. 8 Functions enriched in genes specific to GWSS clusters in all SGBs.
Bar plots show the percentage of genes annotated with selected COG categories and Pfam domains among all genes within GWSS clusters (“Total”) and among genes unique to GWSS clusters (“Sweep specific”) for each SGB. Only COG categories and Pfam domains in SGBs where the difference between “Total” and “Sweep specific” genes was statistically significant (P < 0.000005) are shown, i.e. COG category M (Cell wall/membrane/envelope biogenesis) and COG category L (replication, recombination, and repair). Bars are colored by the disease association status of each GWSS cluster: blue for negative associations, red for positive associations, and gray for no association.
Extended Data Fig. 9 Evaluating the sweep detection and age estimation pipeline with V. cholerae strains from the 7th pandemic.
a. The L2 lineage identified as a distinct clonal GWSS cluster with a clear divergence gap from non-L2 isolates. Each dot represents a mock metagenome generated from a V. cholerae isolate genome. b. Neighbor-joining tree constructed from pairwise distances among mock metagenomes derived from isolates within the L2 lineage and from non-L2 lineages.
Supplementary information
Supplementary Information (download PDF )
Supplementary Figs. 1–4, Supplementary Text Sections 1 and 2 and Supplementary References. The Supplementary Text validates the sweep-calling and sweep-age estimation framework using V. cholerae, a pathogen with well-documented clonal expansions and a known molecular clock rate. The analysis demonstrates that our method can validate that the ongoing seventh cholera pandemic is a GWSS cluster and that the method can recover known pandemic transmission waves and estimate their ages.
